href="/wiki/File:Birds_of_Sweden_2016_07.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Birds_of_Sweden_2016_07.jpg/220px-Birds_of_Sweden_2016_07.jpg" width="220" height="110" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Birds_of_Sweden_2016_07.jpg/330px-Birds_of_Sweden_2016_07.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Birds_of_Sweden_2016_07.jpg/440px-Birds_of_Sweden_2016_07.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1360" data-file-height="680" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Birds_of_Sweden_2016_07.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Common tern in flight</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Birds_of_Sweden_2016_08.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Birds_of_Sweden_2016_08.jpg/220px-Birds_of_Sweden_2016_08.jpg" width="220" height="110" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Birds_of_Sweden_2016_08.jpg/330px-Birds_of_Sweden_2016_08.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Birds_of_Sweden_2016_08.jpg/440px-Birds_of_Sweden_2016_08.jpg 2x" data-file-width="948" data-file-height="474" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Birds_of_Sweden_2016_08.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Common tern in flight</div></div></div>\n<p><b>Terns</b> are <a href="/wiki/Seabird" title="Seabird">seabirds</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Family_(biology)" title="Family (biology)">family</a> <b>Laridae</b> that have a worldwide distribution and are normally found near the <a href="/wiki/Sea" title="Sea">sea</a>, <a href="/wiki/River" title="River">rivers</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Wetland" title="Wetland">wetlands</a>. Terns are treated as a subgroup of the family Laridae which includes <a href="/wiki/Gull" title="Gull">gulls</a> and <a href="/wiki/Skimmer" title="Skimmer">skimmers</a> and consist of eleven <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genera</a>. They are slender, lightly built birds with long, forked tails, narrow wings, long bills, and relatively short legs. Most species are pale grey above and white below, with a contrasting black cap to the head, but the <a href="/wiki/Marsh_tern" title="Marsh tern">marsh terns</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Inca_tern" title="Inca tern">Inca tern</a>, and some <a href="/wiki/Noddy_(tern)" class="mw-redirect" title="Noddy (tern)">noddies</a> have dark <a href="/wiki/Plumage" title="Plumage">plumage</a> for at least part of the year. The sexes are identical in appearance, but young birds are readily distinguishable from adults. Terns have a non-breeding plumage, which usually involves a white forehead and much-reduced black cap.\n</p><p>The terns are birds of open habitats that typically breed in noisy <a href="/wiki/Bird_colony" title="Bird colony">colonies</a> and lay their eggs on bare ground with little or no nest material. Marsh terns construct floating nests from the vegetation in their wetland habitats, and a few species build simple nests in trees, on cliffs or in crevices. The <a href="/wiki/White_tern" title="White tern">white tern</a>, uniquely, lays its single egg on a bare tree branch. Depending on the species, one to three eggs make up the <a href="/wiki/Clutch_(eggs)" title="Clutch (eggs)">clutch</a>. Most species feed on fish caught by diving from flight, but the marsh terns are insect-eaters, and some large terns will supplement their diet with small land <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrates</a>. Many terns are long-distance <a href="/wiki/Bird_migration" title="Bird migration">migrants</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_tern" title="Arctic tern">Arctic tern</a> may see more daylight in a year than any other animal.\n</p><p>Terns are long-lived birds and are relatively free from natural predators and <a href="/wiki/Parasitism" title="Parasitism">parasites</a>; most species are declining in numbers due directly or indirectly to human activities, including habitat loss, pollution, disturbance, and predation by <a href="/wiki/Introduced_species" title="Introduced species">introduced mammals</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Chinese_crested_tern" title="Chinese crested tern">Chinese crested tern</a> is in a <a href="/wiki/Critically_endangered" title="Critically endangered">critical</a> situation and three other species are classed as <a href="/wiki/Endangered_species" title="Endangered species">endangered</a>. International agreements provide a measure of protection, but adults and eggs of some species are still used for food in the tropics. The eggs of two species are eaten in the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">West Indies</a> because they are believed to have <a href="/wiki/Aphrodisiac" title="Aphrodisiac">aphrodisiac</a> properties.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Taxonomy"><span class="toclevel-1 tocsection-5"><a href="#Distribution_and_habitat"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Distribution and habitat</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 class="toctext">Feeding</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-9"><a href="#Predators_and_parasites"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Predators and class="toclevel-1 tocsection-12"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-13"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-14"><a href="#Cited_texts"><span class="tocnumber">9.1</span> <span class="toctext">Cited texts</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-15"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxonomy">Taxonomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tern&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Taxonomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Charadriiformes" title="Charadriiformes">Charadriiformes</a> <a href="/wiki/Order_(biology)" title="Order (biology)">order</a> of birds contains 18 <a href="/wiki/Seabird" title="Seabird">coastal seabird</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wader" title="Wader">wader</a> <a href="/wiki/Family_(biology)" title="Family (biology)">families</a>. Within the order, the terns form a lineage with the <a href="/wiki/Gull" title="Gull">gulls</a>, and, less closely, with the <a href="/wiki/Skimmer" title="Skimmer">skimmers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Skua" title="Skua">skuas</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Auk" title="Auk">auks</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-tuinen_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tuinen-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Early authors such as <a href="/wiki/Conrad_Gessner" title="Conrad Gessner">Conrad Gessner</a>, <a href="/wiki/Francis_Willughby" title="Francis Willughby">Francis Willughby</a>, and <a href="/wiki/William_Turner_(naturalist)" title="William Turner (naturalist)">William Turner</a> did not clearly separate terns from gulls,<sup id="cite_ref-hume12_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hume12-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> but <a href="/wiki/Carl_Linnaeus" title="Carl Linnaeus">Linnaeus</a> recognised the distinction in his 1758 <i><a href="/wiki/Systema_Naturae" title="Systema Naturae">Systema Naturae</a></i>, placing the gulls in the genus <i> <a href="/wiki/Larus" title="Larus">Larus</a></i> and the terns in <i>Sterna</i>. He gave <i>Sterna</i> the description <i>rostrum subulatum</i>, "<a href="/wiki/Bradawl" title="Bradawl">awl-shaped</a> bill", referring to the long, pointed bills typical of this group of birds, a feature that distinguishes them from the thicker-billed gulls.<sup id="cite_ref-linn84_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-linn84-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-job338_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-job338-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-brookes_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brookes-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Behaviour and <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(biology)" title="Morphology (biology)">morphology</a> suggest that the terns are more closely related to the gulls than to the skimmers or skuas, and although <a href="/wiki/Charles_Lucien_Bonaparte" title="Charles Lucien Bonaparte">Charles Lucien Bonaparte</a> created the family Sternidae for the terns in 1838, for many years they were considered to be a subfamily, Sterninae, of the gull family, Laridae. Relationships between various tern species, and between the terns and the other Charadriiformes, were formerly difficult to resolve because of a poor <a href="/wiki/Fossil" title="Fossil">fossil</a> record and the misidentification of some finds.<sup id="cite_ref-HBW_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-pietri_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pietri-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Following <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">genetic</a> research in the early twenty-first century, the terns were historically treated as a separate family: Sternidae.<sup id="cite_ref-paton_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-paton-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Most terns were formerly treated as belonging to one large genus, <i><a href="/wiki/Sterna" title="Sterna">Sterna</a></i>, with just a few dark species placed in other genera; in 1959, only the <a href="/wiki/Noddy_(tern)" class="mw-redirect" title="Noddy (tern)">noddies</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Inca_tern" title="Inca tern">Inca tern</a> were excluded from <i>Sterna</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Moynihan_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moynihan-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-collinson_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-collinson-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> A recent analysis of <a href="/wiki/Nucleic_acid_sequence" title="Nucleic acid sequence">DNA sequences</a> supported the splitting of <i>Sterna</i> into several smaller genera.<sup id="cite_ref-collinson_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-collinson-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bridge_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bridge-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> One study of part of the <a href="/wiki/Cytochrome_b" title="Cytochrome b">cytochrome b</a> gene sequence found a close relationship between terns and a group of waders in the suborder <a href="/wiki/Thinocori" class="mw-redirect" title="Thinocori">Thinocori</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-thomas_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thomas-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> These results are in disagreement with other molecular and morphological studies, and have been interpreted as showing either a large degree of molecular <a href="/wiki/Convergent_evolution" title="Convergent evolution">convergent evolution</a> between the terns and these waders, or the retention of an ancient <a href="/wiki/Genotype" title="Genotype">genotype</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-paton_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-paton-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The word "stearn" was used for these birds in <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> as early as the eighth century, and appears in the poem <i><a href="/wiki/The_Seafarer_(poem)" title="The Seafarer (poem)">The Seafarer</a></i>, written in the ninth century or earlier. Variants such as "tearn" occurred by the eleventh century, although the older form lingered on in <a href="/wiki/Norfolk_dialect" title="Norfolk dialect">Norfolk dialect</a> for several centuries.<sup id="cite_ref-hume12_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hume12-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> As now, the term was used for the inland <a href="/wiki/Black_tern" title="Black tern">black tern</a> as well as the marine species.<sup id="cite_ref-job365_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-job365-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-lock_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lock-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Some authorities consider "tearn" and similar forms to be variants of "stearn",<sup id="cite_ref-hume12_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hume12-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> while others derive the English words from Scandinavian equivalents such as Danish and Norwegian <i>terne</i> or Swedish <i>t\xc3\xa4rna</i>, and ultimately from <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse" title="Old Norse">Old Norse</a> <i>\xc3\xbeerna</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-OED1_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OED1-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Linnaeus adopted "stearn" or "sterna" (which the naturalist <a href="/wiki/William_Turner_(naturalist)" title="William Turner (naturalist)">William Turner</a> had used in 1544 as a Latinisation of an English word, presumably "stern", for the black tern)<sup id="cite_ref-OED2_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OED2-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Turner78_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Turner78-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> or a <a href="/wiki/North_Germanic_languages" title="North Germanic languages">North Germanic</a> equivalent for his genus name <i>Sterna</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-linn155_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-linn155-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Species">Species</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tern&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Species">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Cladogram" title="Cladogram">cladogram</a> shows the relationships between the tern genera, and the currently recognised species, based on <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_DNA" title="Mitochondrial DNA">mitochondrial DNA</a> studies, are listed below:<sup id="cite_ref-bridge_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bridge-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table style="border: 1px solid #ccc; terns\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Fairy_tern" title="Fairy tern">Fairy tern</a> (<i>Sternula nereis</i>)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Damara_tern" title="Damara tern">Damara tern</a> (<i>S. balaenarum</i>)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Little_tern" title="Little tern">Little tern</a> (<i>S. albifrons</i>)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Saunders%27s_tern" title="Saunders&#39;s tern">Saunders\'s tern</a> (<i>S. saundersi</i> formerly considered to be a subspecies of the little tern)<sup id="cite_ref-harrison382_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-harrison382-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Least_tern" title="Least tern">Least tern</a> (<i>S. antillarum</i> formerly considered to be a subspecies of the little tern)<sup id="cite_ref-harrison382_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-harrison382-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Yellow-billed_tern" title="Yellow-billed tern">Yellow-billed tern</a> (<i>S. superciliaris</i>)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Peruvian_tern" title="Peruvian tern">Peruvian tern</a> (<i>S. lorata</i>)</li></ul></li>\n<li>Genus <i>Phaetusa</i> \xe2\x80\x94 large-billed terns\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Large-billed_tern" title="Large-billed tern">Large-billed tern</a> (<i>Phaetusa simplex</i>)</li></ul></li>\n<li>Genus <i>Hydroprogne</i> \xe2\x80\x94 Caspian terns\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Caspian_tern" title="Caspian tern">Caspian tern</a> (<i>Hydroprogne caspia</i>)</li></ul></li></ul>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tern_in_Chilka,_Orissa_I_IMG_9309.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Tern_in_Chilka%2C_Orissa_I_IMG_9309.jpg/220px-Tern_in_Chilka%2C_Orissa_I_IMG_9309.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Tern_in_Chilka%2C_Orissa_I_IMG_9309.jpg/440px-Tern_in_Chilka%2C_Orissa_I_IMG_9309.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tern_in_Chilka,_Orissa_I_IMG_9309.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Gull-billed_tern" title="Gull-billed tern">gull-billed tern</a> will sometimes prey on the chicks and young of other terns</div></div></div>\n<ul><li>Genus <i>Gelochelidon</i> \xe2\x80\x94 gull-billed terns\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Gull-billed_tern" title="Gull-billed tern">Gull-billed tern</a> (<i>Gelochelidon nilotica</i>)</li></ul></li>\n<li>Genus <i>Larosterna</i> \xe2\x80\x94 Inca terns\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Inca_tern" title="Inca tern">Inca tern</a> (<i>Larosterna inca</i>)</li></ul></li></ul>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Chlidonias_hybrida_2_(Marek_Szczepanek).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" data-file-height="420" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Chlidonias_hybrida_2_(Marek_Szczepanek).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Whiskered_tern" title="Whiskered tern">whiskered tern</a> is an insect-eating <a href="/wiki/Marsh_tern" title="Marsh tern">marsh tern</a></div></div></div>\n<ul><li>Genus <i><a href="/wiki/Marsh_tern" title="Marsh tern">Chlidonias</a></i> \xe2\x80\x94 marsh terns\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Black_tern" title="Black tern">Black tern</a> (<i>Chlidonias niger</i>)</li>\n<li><a tern">Whiskered tern</a> (<i>C. hybridus</i>)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Black-fronted_tern" title="Black-fronted tern">Black-fronted tern</a> (<i>C. albostriatus</i> sometimes placed in <i>Sterna</i>)<sup id="cite_ref-birdlifealbostriata_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-birdlifealbostriata-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup></li></ul></li>\n<li>Genus <i><a href="/wiki/Thalasseus" title="Thalasseus">Thalasseus</a></i> \xe2\x80\x94 crested terns\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Lesser_crested_tern" title="Lesser crested tern">Lesser crested tern</a> (<i>Thalasseus bengalensis</i>)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Royal_tern" title="Royal tern">Royal tern</a> (<i>T. maximus</i>)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Greater_crested_tern" title="Greater crested tern">Greater crested tern</a> (or swift tern, <i>T. bergii</i>)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Chinese_crested_tern" title="Chinese crested tern">Chinese crested tern</a> (<i>T. bernsteini</i>)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Elegant_tern" title="Elegant tern">Elegant tern</a> (<i>T. elegans</i>)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Sandwich_tern" title="Sandwich tern">Sandwich tern</a> (<i>T. sandvicensis</i>)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Cabot%27s_tern" title="Cabot&#39;s tern">Cabot\'s tern</a> (<i>T. acuflavidus</i> newly split from <i>T. sandvicensis</i>)<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup></li></ul></li></ul>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a 2x" data-file-width="1002" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:River_Tern_(Sterna_aurantia)-_Adult_%26_Immature_W_IMG_9721.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/River_tern" title="River tern">River tern</a> adult and immatures</div></div></div>\n<ul><li>Genus <i><a href="/wiki/Sterna" title="Sterna">Sterna</a></i> \xe2\x80\x94 large white terns\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Forster%27s_tern" title="Forster&#39;s tern">Forster\'s tern</a> (<i>Sterna forsteri</i>)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Snowy-crowned_tern" title="Snowy-crowned tern">Snowy-crowned tern</a> (<i>S. trudeaui</i>)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Common_tern" title="Common tern">Common tern</a> (<i>S. hirundo</i>)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Roseate_tern" title="Roseate tern">Roseate tern</a> (<i>S. dougallii</i>)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/White-fronted_tern" title="White-fronted tern">White-fronted tern</a> (<i>S. striata</i>)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Black-naped_tern" title="Black-naped tern">Black-naped tern</a> (<i>S. sumatrana</i>)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/South_American_tern" title="South American tern">South American tern</a> (<i>S. hirundinacea</i>)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Antarctic_tern" title="Antarctic tern">Antarctic tern</a> (<i>S. vittata</i>)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Kerguelen_tern" title="Kerguelen tern">Kerguelen tern</a> (<i>S. virgata</i>)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Arctic_tern" title="Arctic tern">Arctic tern</a> (<i>S. paradisaea</i>)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/River_tern" title="River tern">River tern</a> (<i>S. aurantia</i>)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Black-bellied_tern" title="Black-bellied tern">Black-bellied tern</a> (<i>S. acuticauda</i> possibly <i>Chlidonias</i>)<sup id="cite_ref-bridge_13-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bridge-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/White-cheeked_tern" title="White-cheeked tern">White-cheeked tern</a> (<i>S. repressa</i> possibly <i>Chlidonias</i>)<sup id="cite_ref-bridge_13-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bridge-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup></li></ul></li></ul>\n<p>In addition to extant species, the fossil record includes a <a href="/wiki/Miocene" title="Miocene">Miocene</a> <a href="/wiki/Chronospecies" title="Chronospecies">palaeospecies</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Sterna_milne-edwardsii" title="Sterna milne-edwardsii">Sterna milne-edwardsii</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-fossil_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fossil-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The genera <i>Anous</i>, <i>Procelsterna</i> and <i>Gygis</i> are collectively known as noddies, the <i>Chlidonias</i> species are the marsh terns,<sup id="cite_ref-HBW_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> and all other species comprise the sea terns.<sup id="cite_ref-Barlow_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barlow-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-perrins_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-perrins-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Description">Description</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tern&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Description">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Larosterna_inca_(Inca_Tern_-_Inkaseeschwalbe)_Weltvogelpark_Walsrode_2012-015.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Larosterna_inca_%28Inca_Tern_-_Inkaseeschwalbe%29_Weltvogelpark_Walsrode_2012-015.jpg/220px-Larosterna_inca_%28Inca_Tern_-_Inkaseeschwalbe%29_Weltvogelpark_Walsrode_2012-015.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Larosterna_inca_%28Inca_Tern_-_Inkaseeschwalbe%29_Weltvogelpark_Walsrode_2012-015.jpg/330px-Larosterna_inca_%28Inca_Tern_-_Inkaseeschwalbe%29_Weltvogelpark_Walsrode_2012-015.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Larosterna_inca_%28Inca_Tern_-_Inkaseeschwalbe%29_Weltvogelpark_Walsrode_2012-015.jpg/440px-Larosterna_inca_%28Inca_Tern_-_Inkaseeschwalbe%29_Weltvogelpark_Walsrode_2012-015.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="1987" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Larosterna_inca_(Inca_Tern_-_Inkaseeschwalbe)_Weltvogelpark_Walsrode_2012-015.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Inca_tern" title="Inca tern">Inca tern</a>\'s plumage is atypical of the family.</div></div></div>\n<p>Terns range in size from the <a href="/wiki/Least_tern" title="Least tern">least tern</a>, at 23&#160;cm (9.1&#160;in) in length and weighing 30\xe2\x80\x9345&#160;g (1.1\xe2\x80\x931.6&#160;oz),<sup id="cite_ref-Maehr_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maehr-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-olsen136_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-olsen136-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> to the <a href="/wiki/Caspian_tern" title="Caspian tern">Caspian tern</a> at 48\xe2\x80\x9356&#160;cm (19\xe2\x80\x9322&#160;in), 500\xe2\x80\x93700&#160;g (18\xe2\x80\x9325&#160;oz).<sup id="cite_ref-harrisoncasp_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-harrisoncasp-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> They are longer-billed, lighter-bodied, and more streamlined than gulls, and their long tails and long narrow wings give them an elegance in flight. Male and female plumages are identical, although the male can be 2\xe2\x80\x935% larger than the female and often has a relatively larger bill. Sea terns have deeply forked tails, and at least a shallow "V" is shown by all other species.<sup id="cite_ref-HBW_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> The noddies (genera <i>Anous</i>, <i>Procelsterna</i> and <i>Gygis</i>) have unusual notched-wedge shaped tails, the longest tail feathers being the middle-outer, rather than the central or outermost.<sup id="cite_ref-harrison388_22-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-harrison388-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hutton_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hutton-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Although their legs are short, terns can run well. They rarely swim, despite having webbed feet, usually landing on water only to bathe.<sup id="cite_ref-HBW_7-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The majority of sea terns have light grey or white body <a href="/wiki/Plumage" title="Plumage">plumage</a> as adults, with a black cap to the head. The legs and bill are various combinations of red, orange, yellow, or black depending on species. The pale plumage is conspicuous from a distance at sea, and may attract other birds to a good feeding area for these fish-eating species. When seen against the sky, the white underparts also help to hide the hunting bird from its intended prey. The Inca tern has mainly dark plumage, and three species that mainly eat insects, the black tern, <a href="/wiki/White-winged_tern" title="White-winged tern">white-winged tern</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Black-bellied_tern" title="Black-bellied tern">black-bellied tern</a>, have black underparts in the breeding season. The <i>Anous</i> noddies have dark plumage with a pale head cap. The reason for their dark plumage is unknown, but it has been suggested that in tropical areas, where food resources are scarce, the less conspicuous colouration makes it harder for other noddies to detect a feeding bird.<sup id="cite_ref-simmons_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-simmons-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Plumage type, especially the head pattern, is linked to the <a href="/wiki/Phylogenetics" title="Phylogenetics">phylogeny</a> of the terns, and the pale-capped, dark-bodied noddies are believed to have diverged earlier than the other genera from an ancestral white-headed gull, followed by the partially black-headed <i><a href="/wiki/Onychoprion" title="Onychoprion">Onychoprion</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Sternula" title="Sternula">Sternula</a></i> groupings.<sup id="cite_ref-bridge_13-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bridge-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Juvenile terns typically have brown- or yellow-tinged upperparts, and the feathers have dark edges that give the plumage a scaly appearance. They have dark bands on the wings and short tails. In most species, the subsequent <a href="/wiki/Moulting" title="Moulting">moult</a> does not start until after migration, the plumage then becoming more like the adult, but with some retained juvenile feathers and a white forehead with only a partial dark cap. By the second summer, the appearance is very like the adult, and full mature plumage is usually attained by the third year. After breeding, terns moult into a winter plumage, typically showing a white forehead. Heavily worn or aberrant plumages such as <a href="/wiki/Melanism" title="Melanism">melanism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Albinism" title="Albinism">albinism</a> are much rarer in terns than in gulls.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Voice">Voice</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tern&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Voice">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<table role="presentation" class="mbox-small" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td class="mbox-image"><div class="center"><div class="floatnone"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png" width="50" height="50" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="160" data-file-height="160" /></div></div></td>\n<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em"><div class="haudio">\n<div style="padding:4px 0"><a href="/wiki/File:Sterna-hirundo-002.ogg" title="File:Sterna-hirundo-002.ogg">Common tern (80 s)</a></div>\n<div><div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio id="mwe_player_0" controls="" preload="none" style="width:220px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="80.633645833333" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Sterna-hirundo-002.ogg" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/02/Sterna-hirundo-002.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file (88 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="88384" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/0/02/Sterna-hirundo-002.ogg/Sterna-hirundo-002.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-title="MP3" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="152776" /></audio></div></div>\n<div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0"></div></div></td></tr>\n<tr><td colspan="2" class="mbox-text" style="line-height:1.1em"><hr /><span><i>Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Help:Media" title="Help:Media">media help</a>.</i></span></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Terns have a wide repertoire of vocalisations. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Common_tern" title="Common tern">common tern</a> has a distinctive <a href="/wiki/Alarm_signal" title="Alarm signal">alarm</a>, <i>kee-yah</i>, also used as a warning to intruders, and a shorter <i>kyar</i>, given as an individual takes flight in response to a more serious threat; this quietens the usually noisy colony while its residents assess the danger. Other calls include a down-slurred <i>keeur</i> given when an adult is approaching the nest with a fish, and a <i>kip</i> uttered during social contact.<sup id="cite_ref-hume68_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hume68-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Parents and chicks can locate one another by call,<sup id="cite_ref-burton_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-burton-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Sibling" title="Sibling">siblings</a> also recognise each other\'s vocalisations from about the twelfth day after hatching, which helps to keep the brood together.<sup id="cite_ref-sibling_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sibling-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Stevenson_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stevenson-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Vocal differences reinforce species separation between closely related birds such as the least and <a href="/wiki/Little_tern" title="Little tern">little terns</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Massey_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Massey-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> and can help humans distinguish similar species, such as common and <a href="/wiki/Arctic_tern" title="Arctic tern">Arctic terns</a>, since flight calls are unique to each species.<sup id="cite_ref-const_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-const-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-burger_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-burger-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Distribution_and_habitat">Distribution and habitat</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tern&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Distribution and habitat">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Chlidonias_niger.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Chlidonias_niger.jpg/220px-Chlidonias_niger.jpg" width="220" height="125" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d4/Chlidonias_niger.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="235" data-file-height="134" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Chlidonias_niger.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Black_tern" title="Black tern">black tern</a> breeds on inland marshes.</div></div></div>\n<p>Terns have a worldwide distribution, breeding on all continents including Antarctica. The northernmost and southernmost breeders are the Arctic tern and <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_tern" title="Antarctic tern">Antarctic tern</a> respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-HBW_7-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-harrison370_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-harrison370-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Many terns breeding in temperate zones are long-distance <a href="/wiki/Bird_migration" title="Bird migration">migrants</a>, and the Arctic tern probably sees more annual daylight than any other animal as it migrates from its northern breeding grounds to Antarctic waters, a return journey of more than 30,000&#160;km (19,000&#160;mi). A common tern that hatched in Sweden and was found dead five months later on <a href="/wiki/Stewart_Island_/_Rakiura" class="mw-redirect" title="Stewart Island / Rakiura">Stewart Island</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a>, must have flown at least 25,000&#160;km (16,000&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-Newton150_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Newton150-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Actual flight distances are, of course, much greater than the shortest possible route. Arctic terns from Greenland were shown by radio <a href="/wiki/Geolocation" title="Geolocation">geolocation</a> to average 70,000&#160;km (43,000&#160;mi) on their annual migrations.<sup id="cite_ref-Egevan_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Egevan-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Most terns breed on open sandy or rocky areas on coasts and islands. The <a href="/wiki/Yellow-billed_tern" title="Yellow-billed tern">yellow-billed</a>, <a href="/wiki/Large-billed_tern" title="Large-billed tern">large-billed</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Black-fronted_tern" title="Black-fronted tern">black-fronted terns</a> breed only on rivers, and common, least and little terns also sometimes use inland locations. The <a href="/wiki/Marsh_tern" title="Marsh tern">marsh terns</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trudeau%27s_tern" class="mw-redirect" title="Trudeau&#39;s tern">Trudeau\'s tern</a> and some <a href="/wiki/Forster%27s_tern" title="Forster&#39;s tern">Forster\'s terns</a> nest in inland marshes. The <a href="/wiki/Black_noddy" title="Black noddy">black noddy</a> and the <a href="/wiki/White_tern" title="White tern">white tern</a> nest above ground level on cliffs or in trees. Migratory terns move to the coast after breeding, and most species winter near land, although some marine species, like the <a href="/wiki/Aleutian_tern" title="Aleutian tern">Aleutian tern</a>, may wander far from land. The <a href="/wiki/Sooty_tern" title="Sooty tern">sooty tern</a> is entirely oceanic when not breeding, and healthy young birds are not seen on land for up to five years after fledging until they return to breed. They lack waterproof plumage, so they cannot rest on the sea. Where they spend the years prior to breeding is unknown.<sup id="cite_ref-HBW_7-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Behaviour">Behaviour</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tern&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Behaviour">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Breeding">Breeding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tern&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Breeding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Starr_080605-6653_Casuarina_equisetifolia.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Starr_080605-6653_Casuarina_equisetifolia.jpg/220px-Starr_080605-6653_Casuarina_equisetifolia.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Starr_080605-6653_Casuarina_equisetifolia.jpg/330px-Starr_080605-6653_Casuarina_equisetifolia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Starr_080605-6653_Casuarina_equisetifolia.jpg/440px-Starr_080605-6653_Casuarina_equisetifolia.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2816" data-file-height="2112" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Starr_080605-6653_Casuarina_equisetifolia.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Brown_noddy" title="Brown noddy">Brown noddy</a> nest on stump of <i><a href="/wiki/Casuarina_equisetifolia" title="Casuarina equisetifolia">Casuarina equisetifolia</a></i></div></div></div>\n<p>Terns are normally <a href="/wiki/Monogamy" title="Monogamy">monogamous</a>, although trios or female-female pairings have been observed in at least three species.<sup id="cite_ref-HBW_7-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nisbet_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nisbet-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Most terns breed annually and at the same time of year, but some tropical species may nest at intervals shorter than 12 months or <a href="/wiki/Synchronization" title="Synchronization">asynchronously</a>. Most terns become sexually mature when aged three, although some small species may breed in their second year. Some large sea terns, including the sooty and <a href="/wiki/Bridled_tern" title="Bridled tern">bridled terns</a>, are four or older when they first breed. Terns normally breed in <a href="/wiki/Bird_colony" title="Bird colony">colonies</a>, and are <a href="/wiki/Philopatry" title="Philopatry">site-faithful</a> if their habitat is sufficiently stable. A few species nest in small or dispersed groups, but most breed in colonies of up to a few hundred pairs, often alongside other seabirds such as gulls or skimmers.<sup id="cite_ref-HBW_7-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Large tern species tend to form larger colonies,<sup id="cite_ref-burger_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-burger-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> which in the case of the sooty tern can contain up to two million pairs. Large species nest very close together and sit tightly, making it difficult for aerial predators to land among them. Smaller species are less closely packed and <a href="/wiki/Mobbing_(animal_behavior)" title="Mobbing (animal behavior)">mob</a> intruders. <a href="/wiki/Peruvian_tern" title="Peruvian tern">Peruvian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Damara_tern" title="Damara tern">Damara terns</a> have small dispersed colonies and rely on the <a href="/wiki/Crypsis" title="Crypsis">cryptic</a> plumage of the eggs and young for protection.<sup id="cite_ref-HBW_7-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The male selects a <a href="/wiki/Territory_(animal)" title="Territory (animal)">territory</a>, which he defends against <a href="/wiki/Conspecificity" class="mw-redirect" title="Conspecificity">conspecifics</a>, and re-establishes a <a href="/wiki/Pair_bond" title="Pair bond">pair bond</a> with his mate or attracts a new female if necessary. Courtship involves ritualised flight and ground displays, and the male often presents a fish to his partner. Most species have little or no nest, laying the eggs onto bare ground, but Trudeau\'s tern, Forster\'s tern and the marsh terns construct floating nests from the vegetation in their wetland habitats. Black and <a href="/wiki/Lesser_noddy" title="Lesser noddy">lesser noddies</a> build nests of twigs, feathers and excreta on tree branches, and <a href="/wiki/Brown_noddy" title="Brown noddy">brown</a>, <a href="/wiki/Blue_noddy" title="Blue noddy">blue</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Grey_noddy" title="Grey noddy">grey noddies</a> make rough platforms of grass and seaweed on cliff ledges, in cavities or on other rocky surfaces.<sup id="cite_ref-HBW_7-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-watling206_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-watling206-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> The Inca tern nests in crevices, caves and disused burrows, such as that of a <a href="/wiki/Humboldt_penguin" title="Humboldt penguin">Humboldt penguin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-birdlifeinca_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-birdlifeinca-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> The white tern is unique in that it lays its single egg on a bare tree branch.<sup id="cite_ref-NSWNPWS_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NSWNPWS-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Tropical species usually lay just one egg, but two or three is typical in cooler regions if there is an adequate food supply. The time taken to complete the <a href="/wiki/Clutch_(eggs)" title="Clutch (eggs)">clutch</a> varies, but for temperate species <a href="/wiki/Egg_incubation" title="Egg incubation">incubation</a> takes 21\xe2\x80\x9328&#160;days.<sup id="cite_ref-HBW_7-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> The eggs of most gulls and terns are brown\nwith dark splotches, so they are difficult for predators to spot on the beach.<sup id="cite_ref-burger_42-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-burger-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Precocial" class="mw-redirect" title="Precocial">precocial</a> chicks <a href="/wiki/Fledge" title="Fledge">fledge</a> in about four weeks after hatching. Tropical species take longer because of the poorer food supply. Both parents incubate the eggs and feed the chicks, although the female does more incubating and less fishing than her partner.<sup id="cite_ref-HBW_7-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Young birds migrate with the adults.<sup id="cite_ref-burger_42-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-burger-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Terns are generally long-lived birds, with individuals typically returning for 7\xe2\x80\x9310 breeding seasons. Maximum known ages include 34 for an Arctic tern and 32 for a sooty. Although several other species are known to live in captivity for up to 20 years, their greatest recorded ages are underestimates because the birds can outlive their <a href="/wiki/Bird_ringing" title="Bird ringing">rings</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-HBW_7-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Interbreeding between tern species is rare, and involves closely related species when it occurs. Hybrids recorded include common tern with roseate, Sandwich with lesser-crested, and black with white-winged.<sup id="cite_ref-olsen110_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-olsen110-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Feeding">Feeding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tern&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Feeding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sterna_hirundo_-Nantucket_National_Wildlife_Refuge,_Massachusetts,_USA_-adult_and_juvenile-8.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Sterna_hirundo_-Nantucket_National_Wildlife_Refuge%2C_Massachusetts%2C_USA_-adult_and_juvenile-8.jpg/220px-Sterna_hirundo_-Nantucket_National_Wildlife_Refuge%2C_Massachusetts%2C_USA_-adult_and_juvenile-8.jpg" width="220" height="161" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Sterna_hirundo_-Nantucket_National_Wildlife_Refuge%2C_Massachusetts%2C_USA_-adult_and_juvenile-8.jpg/330px-Sterna_hirundo_-Nantucket_National_Wildlife_Refuge%2C_Massachusetts%2C_USA_-adult_and_juvenile-8.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Sterna_hirundo_-Nantucket_National_Wildlife_Refuge%2C_Massachusetts%2C_USA_-adult_and_juvenile-8.jpg/440px-Sterna_hirundo_-Nantucket_National_Wildlife_Refuge%2C_Massachusetts%2C_USA_-adult_and_juvenile-8.jpg 2x" data-file-width="720" data-file-height="527" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sterna_hirundo_-Nantucket_National_Wildlife_Refuge,_Massachusetts,_USA_-adult_and_juvenile-8.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An adult <a href="/wiki/Common_tern" title="Common tern">common tern</a> bringing a <a href="/wiki/Sand_eel" title="Sand eel">sand eel</a> to a juvenile</div></div></div>\n<p>Most terns hunt fish by diving, often hovering first, and the particular approach technique used can help to distinguish similar species at a distance.<sup id="cite_ref-humepearson_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-humepearson-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Sea terns often hunt in association with <a href="/wiki/Porpoise" title="Porpoise">porpoises</a> or predatory fish, such as <a href="/wiki/Bluefish" title="Bluefish">bluefish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tuna" title="Tuna">tuna</a> or <a href="/wiki/Bonito" title="Bonito">bonitos</a>, since these large marine animals drive the prey to the surface. Sooty terns feed at night as the fish rise to the surface, and are believed to sleep on the wing since they become waterlogged easily. Terns of several species will feed on <a href="/wiki/Invertebrate" title="Invertebrate">invertebrates</a>, following the plough or hunting on foot on <a href="/wiki/Mudflat" title="Mudflat">mudflats</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-HBW_7-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> The marsh terns normally catch insects in the air or pick them off the surface of fresh water. Other species will sometimes use these techniques if the opportunity arises.<sup id="cite_ref-collins_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-collins-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> An individual tern\'s foraging efficiency increases with its age.<sup id="cite_ref-burger_42-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-burger-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Gull-billed_tern" title="Gull-billed tern">gull-billed tern</a> is an opportunist predator, taking a wide variety of prey from marine, freshwater and terrestrial habitats. Depending on what is available it will eat small crabs, fish, <a href="/wiki/Crayfish" title="Crayfish">crayfish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grasshopper" title="Grasshopper">grasshoppers</a> and other large insects, lizards and <a href="/wiki/Amphibian" title="Amphibian">amphibians</a>. <a href="/wiki/Warm-blooded" title="Warm-blooded">Warm-blooded</a> prey includes <a href="/wiki/Mouse" title="Mouse">mice</a> and the eggs and chicks of other beach-breeding birds; least terns, little terns and members of its own species may be victims.<sup id="cite_ref-qld_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-qld-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Erwin_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Erwin-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-dies_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dies-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Greater_crested_tern" title="Greater crested tern">greater crested tern</a> will also occasionally catch unusual <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrate</a> species such as <a href="/wiki/Agamidae" title="Agamidae">agamid lizards</a> and <a href="/wiki/Green_sea_turtle" title="Green sea turtle">green sea turtle</a> hatchlings, and follows trawlers for discards.<sup id="cite_ref-Blaber_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blaber-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The eyes of terns cannot <a href="/wiki/Accommodation_(eye)" title="Accommodation (eye)">accommodate</a> under water, so they rely on accurate sighting from the air before they plunge-dive.<sup id="cite_ref-Coles_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Coles-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> Like other seabirds that feed at the surface or dive for food, terns have red oil droplets in the <a href="/wiki/Cone_(eye)" class="mw-redirect" title="Cone (eye)">cones</a> of their <a href="/wiki/Retina" title="Retina">retinas</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-Sinclair_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sinclair-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> birds that have to look through an air/water interface have more deeply coloured <a href="/wiki/Carotenoid" title="Carotenoid">carotenoid</a> <a href="/wiki/Pigment" title="Pigment">pigments</a> in the oil drops than other species.<sup id="cite_ref-Varela_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Varela-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> The pigment also improves visual contrast and sharpens distance vision, especially in hazy conditions,<sup id="cite_ref-Sinclair_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sinclair-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> and helps terns to locate shoals of fish, although it is uncertain whether they are sighting the <a href="/wiki/Phytoplankton" title="Phytoplankton">phytoplankton</a> on which the fish feed, or other feeding birds.<sup id="cite_ref-Lythgoe_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lythgoe-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> The red colouring reduces <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a> sensitivity, which in any case is an adaptation more suited to terrestrial feeders like the gulls,<sup id="cite_ref-Hastad_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hastad-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> and this protects the eye from UV damage.<sup id="cite_ref-Coles_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Coles-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Predators_and_parasites">Predators and parasites</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tern&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Predators and parasites">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Western_Gull_chasing_Elegant_Tern.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Western_Gull_chasing_Elegant_Tern.jpg/220px-Western_Gull_chasing_Elegant_Tern.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Western_Gull_chasing_Elegant_Tern.jpg/330px-Western_Gull_chasing_Elegant_Tern.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Western_Gull_chasing_Elegant_Tern.jpg/440px-Western_Gull_chasing_Elegant_Tern.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3008" data-file-height="2000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Western_Gull_chasing_Elegant_Tern.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Kleptoparasitism" title="Kleptoparasitism">Kleptoparasitic</a> <a href="/wiki/Western_gull" title="Western gull">western gull</a> chasing <a href="/wiki/Elegant_tern" title="Elegant tern">elegant tern</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The inaccessibility of many tern colonies gave them a measure of protection from <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammalian</a> predators, especially on islands, but <a href="/wiki/Introduced_species" title="Introduced species">introduced species</a> brought by humans can seriously affect breeding birds. These can be predators such as <a href="/wiki/Fox" title="Fox">foxes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Raccoon" title="Raccoon">raccoons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cat" title="Cat">cats</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rat" title="Rat">rats</a>, or animals that destroy the habitat, including <a href="/wiki/Rabbit" title="Rabbit">rabbits</a>, <a href="/wiki/Goat" title="Goat">goats</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pig" title="Pig">pigs</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-HBW_7-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Problems arise not only on formerly mammal-free islands, as in New Zealand, but also where an alien <a href="/wiki/Carnivore" title="Carnivore">carnivore</a>, such as the <a href="/wiki/American_mink" title="American mink">American mink</a> in <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>, presents an unfamiliar threat.<sup id="cite_ref-hume112_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hume112-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Adult terns may be hunted by <a href="/wiki/Owl" title="Owl">owls</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bird_of_prey" title="Bird of prey">raptors</a>, and their chicks and eggs may be taken by <a href="/wiki/Heron" title="Heron">herons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crow" title="Crow">crows</a> or gulls.<sup id="cite_ref-HBW_7-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-qld_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-qld-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> Less obvious nest predators include <a href="/wiki/Ruddy_turnstone" title="Ruddy turnstone">ruddy turnstones</a> in the Arctic, and gull-billed terns in little tern colonies.<sup id="cite_ref-qld_53-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-qld-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-parkes_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-parkes-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Adults may be robbed of their catch by avian <a href="/wiki/Kleptoparasitism" title="Kleptoparasitism">kleptoparasites</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Frigatebird" title="Frigatebird">frigatebirds</a>, skuas, other terns or large gulls.<sup id="cite_ref-HBW_7-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-harrison320_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-harrison320-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>External <a href="/wiki/Parasitism" title="Parasitism">parasites</a> include <a href="/wiki/Mallophaga" title="Mallophaga">chewing lice</a> of the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Saemundssonia" title="Saemundssonia">Saemundssonia</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-ward_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ward-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bird_louse" title="Bird louse">feather lice</a> and fleas such as <i><a href="/wiki/Ceratophyllus_borealis" title="Ceratophyllus borealis">Ceratophyllus borealis</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Rothschild82130_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rothschild82130-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> Lice are often host specific, and the closely related common and Arctic terns carry quite different species.<sup id="cite_ref-Rothschild135_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rothschild135-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Internal parasites include the <a href="/wiki/Crustacean" title="Crustacean">crustacean</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Reighardia_sternae" title="Reighardia sternae">Reighardia sternae</a></i>, and <a href="/wiki/Cestoda" title="Cestoda">tapeworms</a> such as <i><a href="/wiki/Ligula_intestinalis" title="Ligula intestinalis">Ligula intestinalis</a></i> and members of the genera <i><a href="/wiki/Diphyllobothrium" title="Diphyllobothrium">Diphyllobothrium</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Schistocephalus" title="Schistocephalus">Schistocephalus</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Rothschild194_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rothschild194-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> Terns are normally free of blood parasites, unlike gulls that often carry <i><a href="/wiki/Haemoproteus" title="Haemoproteus">Haemoproteus</a></i> species. An exception is the brown noddy, which sometimes harbours <a href="/wiki/Protozoa" title="Protozoa">protozoa</a> of that genus.<sup id="cite_ref-Quillfeldt_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Quillfeldt-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> In 1961 the common tern was the first wild bird species identified as being infected with avian influenza, the <a href="/wiki/Influenza_A_virus_subtype_H5N3" title="Influenza A virus subtype H5N3">H5N3</a> variant being found in an outbreak involving South African birds.<sup id="cite_ref-olsen2006_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-olsen2006-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Several species of terns have been implicated as carriers of <a href="/wiki/West_Nile_virus" title="West Nile virus">West Nile virus</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Takken_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Takken-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Relationships_with_humans">Relationships with humans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tern&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Relationships with humans">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Roseate_Tern_portrait.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Roseate_Tern_portrait.jpg/220px-Roseate_Tern_portrait.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Roseate_Tern_portrait.jpg/330px-Roseate_Tern_portrait.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Roseate_Tern_portrait.jpg/440px-Roseate_Tern_portrait.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Roseate_Tern_portrait.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Roseate_tern" title="Roseate tern">roseate tern</a> is trapped for food on its wintering grounds.</div></div></div>\n<p>Terns and their eggs have long been eaten by humans and island colonies were raided by sailors on long voyages since the eggs or large chicks were an easily obtained source of <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a>. Eggs are still illegally harvested in southern Europe, and adults of wintering birds are taken as food in West Africa and South America. The roseate tern is significantly affected by this hunting, with adult survival 10% lower than would otherwise be expected. In the West Indies, the eggs of roseate and sooty terns are believed to be <a href="/wiki/Aphrodisiac" title="Aphrodisiac">aphrodisiacs</a>, and are disproportionately targeted by egg collectors. Tern skins and feathers have long been used for making items of clothing such as capes and hats, and this became a large-scale activity in the second half of the nineteenth century when it became fashionable to use feathers in <a href="/wiki/Hatmaking" title="Hatmaking">hatmaking</a>. This trend started in Europe but soon spread to the Americas and Australia. White was the preferred colour, and sometimes wings or entire birds were used.<sup id="cite_ref-HBW_7-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-spenn_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-spenn-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Terns have sometimes benefited from human activities, following the plough or fishing boats for easy food supplies, although some birds get trapped in nets or swallow plastic. Fishermen looked for feeding tern flocks, since the birds could lead them to fish shoals. <a href="/wiki/Overfishing" title="Overfishing">Overfishing</a> of small fish such as <a href="/wiki/Sand_eel" title="Sand eel">sand eels</a> can lead to steep declines in the colonies relying on these prey items. More generally, the loss or disruption to tern colonies caused by human activities has caused declines in many species.<sup id="cite_ref-HBW_7-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Pollution has been a problem in some areas, and in the 1960s and 1970s <a href="/wiki/DDT" title="DDT">DDT</a> caused egg loss through thinning of the shells. In the 1980s, <a href="/wiki/Organochloride" title="Organochloride">organochlorides</a> caused severe declines in the <a href="/wiki/Great_Lakes" title="Great Lakes">Great Lakes</a> area of the US.<sup id="cite_ref-burger_42-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-burger-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Because of their sensitivity to pollutants, terns are sometimes used as indicators of contamination levels.<sup id="cite_ref-HBW_7-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Habitat enhancements used to increase the breeding success of terns include floating nest platforms for black, common and Caspian terns,<sup id="cite_ref-shealer_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shealer-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-dunlop_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dunlop-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-lampman_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lampman-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> and artificial islands created for a number of different species.<sup id="cite_ref-burgess_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-burgess-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-krogh_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-krogh-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> More specialised interventions include providing nest boxes for roseate terns, which normally nest in the shelter of tallish vegetation,<sup id="cite_ref-morrisson_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-morrisson-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> and using artificial <a href="/wiki/Zostera" title="Zostera">eelgrass</a> mats to encourage common terns to nest in areas not vulnerable to flooding.<sup id="cite_ref-Palestis_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Palestis-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Conservation_status">Conservation status</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tern&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Conservation status">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ranganathittu_3.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Ranganathittu_3.jpg/220px-Ranganathittu_3.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Ranganathittu_3.jpg/330px-Ranganathittu_3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b9/Ranganathittu_3.jpg 2x" data-file-width="360" data-file-height="240" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ranganathittu_3.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Black-bellied_tern" title="Black-bellied tern">black-bellied tern</a> is <a href="/wiki/Endangered_species" title="Endangered species">endangered</a> by human activities.</div></div></div>\n<p>A number of terns face serious threats, and the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_crested_tern" title="Chinese crested tern">Chinese crested tern</a> is classed as "<a href="/wiki/Critically_endangered" title="Critically endangered">critically endangered</a>" by <a href="/wiki/BirdLife_International" title="BirdLife International">BirdLife International</a>. It has a population of fewer than 50&#160;birds and a breeding range of just 9&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (3.5&#160;mi<sup>2</sup>). It is declining due to egg collection, human disturbance and the loss of coastal wetlands in China.<sup id="cite_ref-birdlifechinese_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-birdlifechinese-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Three other species are categorised as "<a href="/wiki/Endangered_species" title="Endangered species">endangered</a>", with declining populations of less than 10,000 birds. The South Asian black-bellied tern is threatened by habitat loss, egg collecting for food, pollution and predation.<sup id="cite_ref-birdlifeacuticauda_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-birdlifeacuticauda-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> In New Zealand, the black-fronted tern is facing a rapid fall in numbers due to predation by introduced mammals and <a href="/wiki/Australian_magpie" title="Australian magpie">Australian magpies</a>. Disturbance by cattle and sheep and by human activities is also a factor.<sup id="cite_ref-birdlifealbostriata_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-birdlifealbostriata-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The Peruvian tern was initially damaged by the collapse of <a href="/wiki/Peruvian_anchoveta" title="Peruvian anchoveta">anchoveta</a> stocks in 1972, but breeding colonies have subsequently been lost due to building, disturbance and pollution in their coastal wetlands.<sup id="cite_ref-birdlifelorata_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-birdlifelorata-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Australasia" title="Australasia">Australasian</a> <a href="/wiki/Fairy_tern" title="Fairy tern">fairy tern</a> is described as "<a href="/wiki/Vulnerable_species" title="Vulnerable species">vulnerable</a>". Disturbance by humans, dogs and vehicles, predation by introduced species and inappropriate water level management in <a href="/wiki/South_Australia" title="South Australia">South Australia</a> are the main reasons for its decline.<sup id="cite_ref-birdlifenereis_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-birdlifenereis-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> Five species are "<a href="/wiki/Near_threatened" class="mw-redirect" title="Near threatened">near threatened</a>", indicating less severe concerns or only potential vulnerability. The <a href="/wiki/Elegant_tern" title="Elegant tern">elegant tern</a> is so categorised because 95% of the population breeds on one island, <a href="/wiki/San_Lorenzo_Marine_Archipelago_National_Park" title="San Lorenzo Marine Archipelago National Park">Isla Rasa</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_California" title="Gulf of California">Gulf of California</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Kerguelen_tern" title="Kerguelen tern">Kerguelen tern</a> has a population of less than 5,000 adults breeding on small and often stormy Pacific islands.<sup id="cite_ref-birdlifevirgata_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-birdlifevirgata-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-birdlifeelegans_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-birdlifeelegans-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> Three species, the Inca, Damara, and <a href="/wiki/River_tern" title="River tern">river terns</a>, are expected to decline in the future due to habitat loss and disturbance.<sup id="cite_ref-birdlifeinca_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-birdlifeinca-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-birdliferiver_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-birdliferiver-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-birdlifedam_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-birdlifedam-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> Some tern <a href="/wiki/Subspecies" title="Subspecies">subspecies</a> are endangered, including the <a href="/wiki/California_least_tern" title="California least tern">California least tern</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Easter_Island" title="Easter Island">Easter Island</a> race of the grey noddy.<sup id="cite_ref-HBW_7-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Most tern species are declining in numbers due to the loss or disturbance of breeding habitat, pollution and increased predation. Gull populations have increased over the last century because of reduced persecution and the availability of food from human activities, and terns have been forced out of many traditional nesting areas by the larger birds. A few species are defying the trend and showing local increases, including the Arctic tern in <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavia</a>, Forster\'s tern around the Great Lakes, the <a href="/wiki/Sandwich_tern" title="Sandwich tern">Sandwich tern</a> in eastern North America and its yellow-billed subspecies, the Cayenne tern, in the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">Caribbean</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-HBW_7-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Terns are protected by international legislation such as the <a href="/wiki/Agreement_on_the_Conservation_of_African-Eurasian_Migratory_Waterbirds" title="Agreement on the Conservation of African-Eurasian Migratory Waterbirds">Agreement on the Conservation of African-Eurasian Migratory Waterbirds</a> (AEWA) and the US-Canada <a href="/wiki/Migratory_Bird_Treaty_Act_of_1918" title="Migratory Bird Treaty Act of 1918">Migratory Bird Treaty Act of 1918</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-AEWA_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AEWA-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-1918treaty_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1918treaty-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> Parties to the AWEA agreement are required to engage in a wide range of conservation strategies described in a detailed action plan. The plan is intended to address key issues such as species and habitat conservation, management of human activities, research, education, and implementation.<sup id="cite_ref-AEWAintro_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AEWAintro-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> The North American legislation is similar, although there is a greater emphasis on protection.<sup id="cite_ref-law_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-law-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tern&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Seabird_breeding_behavior" title="Seabird breeding behavior">Seabird breeding behavior</a></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tern&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">\n<ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal">Paton, Tara A; Baker, Allan J; Groth, Jeffrey G; Barrowclough, George F (2003).
